(1, 0, 0, 1, 0) PREFACE
(1, 0, 1, 2, 8) A word as to the origin of this work may not be out of place.
(1, 0, 1, 3, 70) It was my great good fortune to live for more than four years, from August 1942 till the end of 1946, at Sri Ramanasramam and to have the inestimable benefit of daily contact with our Bhagavan.
(1, 0, 1, 4, 264) After I had been there for some months various people who visited the Asramam began to suggest that it would be a good thing if I recorded Bhagavans utterances on spiritual topics, either in answer to questions from visitors or in any other context.
(1, 0, 1, 5, 514) For a long time, however, I was too lazy to make the effort.
(1, 0, 1, 6, 575) Nevertheless, whenever I read the Gospel of Sri Ramakrishna Paramahamsa, as I used to now and then, I felt how desirable it was that a similar book should be compiled in the case of our Bhagavan.
(1, 0, 1, 7, 771) When I had drifted on like this for some years, on the morning of January 1st, 1945, within the space of about an hour, three different persons  a lawyers clerk, son of Sankara Ammal doing service in the Asramam, a businessman of Madras by name M.V.P. Sastri, whom I had known since his boyhood at Chittoor, and O.P. Ramaswami Reddi, who later became Chief Minister of Madras  all urged me to undertake the task as, in their kind opinion, I was best fitted for it besides having the necessary facility of constant close contact with Bhagavan.
(1, 0, 2, 8, 1314) The appeal coming from such widely different persons on the first day of the year and all of them approaching me in such quick succession and without any previous consultation amongst themselves made such a deep impression on me that I took it as a call really from Bhagavan.
(1, 0, 2, 9, 1590) So, that very day I started keeping an Asramam Diary.
(1, 0, 2, 10, 1644) My idea was to preserve for Bhagavans devotees all that took place in the Asramam
(1, 1, 0, 1, 1726) that might be of interest or importance from one point of view or another, and mainly Bhagavans answers to questions addressed to him by visitors from all quarters of the globe, for many of whom I acted as a sort of official translator in Bhagavans Court.
(1, 1, 1, 2, 1982) I told Bhagavan of the circumstances under which I started the work, thus asking for his blessing on it.
(1, 1, 1, 3, 2087) Then I obtained permission from the Sarvadhikari.
(1, 1, 1, 4, 2137) Also, for the first few days I read out to Bhagavan whatever I had recorded so that he could correct me anywhere where I had gone wrong.
(1, 1, 1, 5, 2274) Even when I was interpreting Bhagavan to visitors, if I made the slightest mistake Bhagavan would pull me up.
(1, 1, 1, 6, 2384) Whenever I myself was in doubt as to what Bhagavan meant I used to ask for further clarification and Bhagavan willingly explained things again for my benefit.
(1, 1, 1, 7, 2543) After the first two or three days I gave up reading my entries to Bhagavan daily, but on any day when I was in doubt whether I had accurately recorded what Bhagavan had said, I used to read out my entry for the day and correct it wherever he indicated that it was necessary.
(1, 1, 2, 8, 2818) Only a part of these records of mine seems to be available to the Asramam authorities for publication just now.
(1, 1, 2, 9, 2930) I am glad that at least so much is to be published by them immediately.
(1, 1, 2, 10, 3002) I believe they came into existence because Bhagavan willed it and I believe it is his will that at least a part of them should now appear in print.
(1, 1, 2, 11, 3150) I hope and pray to Bhagavan that the publication may prove not merely of interest but of great use to those who read it and that he may bestow his grace on all who go through it in earnestness and faith.
(1, 1, 3, 12, 3354) 1st January 1952 A. DEVARAJA MUDALIAR
(1, 2, 0, 1, 3392) Day by Day with Bhagavan
(1, 2, 1, 2, 3417) ________
(1, 2, 2, 3, 3426) 111116 3 45 Mor 6 3 45 Mor 6 3 45 Mor 6 3 45 Mor 6 3 45 Morning ning ning ning ning
(1, 2, 3, 4, 3510) A visitor: Should I give up my business and take to reading books on Vedanta?
(1, 2, 4, 5, 3588) Bhagavan: If the objects have an independent existence, i.e., if they exist anywhere apart from you, then it may be possible for you to go away from them.
(1, 2, 4, 6, 3743) But they dont exist apart from you; they owe their existence to you, your thought.
(1, 2, 4, 7, 3826) So, where can you go, to escape them?
(1, 2, 4, 8, 3864) As for reading books on Vedanta, you may go on reading any number of them.
(1, 2, 4, 9, 3939) They can only tell you, Realise the Self within you.
(1, 2, 4, 10, 3992) The Self cannot be found in books.
(1, 2, 4, 11, 4027) You have to find it out for yourself, in yourself.
(1, 2, 5, 12, 4078) Ev Ev Ev Ev Evening ening ening ening ening
(1, 2, 6, 13, 4122) Almost the same question was put by another visitor in the afternoon and Bhagavan said, Where can you go, fleeing from the world or objects?
(1, 2, 6, 14, 4263) They are like the shadow of a man, which the man cannot flee from.
(1, 2, 6, 15, 4330) There is a funny story of a man who wanted to bury his shadow.
(1, 2, 6, 16, 4393) He dug a deep pit and, seeing his shadow at the bottom, was glad he could bury it so deep.
(1, 2, 6, 17, 4484) He went on filling the pit and when he had completely filled it up he was surprised and disappointed to find the shadow on top.
(1, 2, 6, 18, 4612) Even so, the objects or thoughts of them will be with you always, till you realise the Self.
(1, 3, 0, 1, 4705) 111117 3 45 Af 7 3 45 Af 7 3 45 Af 7 3 45 Af 7 3 45 Afttttter er er er ernoon noon noon noon noon
(1, 3, 1, 2, 4803) Mr. T.P. Ramachandra Aiyar asked Bhagavan about the meaning of Ao I in the stanza of Ds[ StT (Reality in Forty Verses).
(1, 3, 2, 3, 4923) B: Ao I means \kR I.
(1, 3, 2, 4, 4944) It refers to that light of manas in which we see all the world, both the known and the unknown of the world.
(1, 3, 2, 5, 5053) There is first the white light, so to call it, of the Self, which transcends both light and darkness.
(1, 3, 2, 6, 5155) In it no object can be seen.
(1, 3, 2, 7, 5184) There is neither seer nor seen.
(1, 3, 2, 8, 5216) Then there is total darkness or avidya in which also no objects are seen.
(1, 3, 2, 9, 5290) But from the Self proceeds a reflected light, the light of pure manas, and it is this light which gives room for the existence of all the film of the world which is seen neither in total light nor in total darkness, but only in the subdued or reflected light.
(1, 3, 2, 10, 5550) It is this light which is referred to in the stanza.
(1, 3, 3, 11, 5603) 111118 3 45 8 3 45 8 3 45 8 3 45 8 3 45
(1, 3, 4, 12, 5643) On or about 15 3 45 Bhagavan had asked someone in the hall to read aloud Bhakta Vijayam, to illustrate from the story of Tulasi Das, how one totally immersed in sensual life, suddenly recoils and goes to the other extreme of a highly religious life.
(1, 3, 4, 13, 5893) In the story, Tulasi Das runs away from wife and home and is mad after Hari at Banaras.
(1, 3, 4, 14, 5981) The wife and mother go and entreat him to come back, reminding him of his great love for them all.
(1, 3, 4, 15, 6080) He takes no notice of them at all, but asks them, Has my Hari come?
(1, 3, 4, 16, 6148) He is coming there! etc.
(1, 3, 4, 17, 6173) He was mad after Hari alone and took interest in nothing else.
(1, 3, 4, 18, 6236) When this portion was being read out, Bhagavan said, I was somewhat like this at Madura.
(1, 3, 4, 19, 6325) Going to school, books in hand, I would be eagerly desiring and expecting that God would suddenly appear before me in the sky; and so I would be looking up at the sky.
(1, 3, 4, 20, 6493) What sort of progress could such a one make in his studies at school!
(1, 4, 0, 1, 6563) [This was apparently shortly before he left Madura.
(1, 4, 0, 2, 6615) I have never heard before, either from Bhagavan or from others, that he was so God mad at Madura.
(1, 4, 0, 3, 6713) So I record it here.]
(1, 4, 1, 4, 6735) 111119 3 45 Mor 9 3 45 Mor 9 3 45 Mor 9 3 45 Mor 9 3 45 Morning ning ning ning ning
(1, 4, 2, 5, 6819) A visitor from Sind, very probably Kundanlal A. Mahatani of Hyderabad, Sind, (now Pakistan) asked: It is said the world and the objects that we see are all unreal, like the snake in the rope.
(1, 4, 2, 6, 7011) It is also stated in other places that the seer and the seen are the same.
(1, 4, 2, 7, 7086) If the seer and the seen are same, then how can we say that the seen is unreal?
(1, 4, 3, 8, 7166) B.: All that is meant is that the seen regarded as an independent entity, independent of the Self, is unreal.
(1, 4, 3, 9, 7276) The seen is not different from the seer.
(1, 4, 3, 10, 7317) What exists is the one Self, not a seer and a seen.
(1, 4, 3, 11, 7369) The seen regarded as the Self is real.
(1, 4, 4, 12, 7408) V.: It is said the world is like a dream.
(1, 4, 4, 13, 7450) But there is this difference between dream and the waking state.
(1, 4, 4, 14, 7515) In dream I see my friends or relations and go through some experiences with them.
(1, 4, 4, 15, 7597) When I wake up and ask those friends or relations whom I met in the dream about the dream, they know nothing about it.
(1, 4, 4, 16, 7716) But in the waking state what I see and hear is corroborated by so many others.
(1, 4, 5, 17, 7795) B.: You should not mix up the dream and the waking states.
(1, 4, 5, 18, 7854) Just as you seek corroboration about the waking state experiences from those whom you see in the waking state, you must ask for corroboration about the dream experiences from those whom you saw in the dream state, i.e., when you were in the dream.
(1, 4, 5, 19, 8102) Then in the dream, those friends or relations whom you saw in the dream would corroborate you.
(1, 4, 6, 20, 8197) The main point is, are you prepared when awake to affirm the reality of any of your dream experiences?
(1, 4, 6, 21, 8300) Similarly, one who has awakened into jnana cannot affirm the reality of the waking experience.
(1, 4, 6, 22, 8395) From his viewpoint, the waking state is dream.
(1, 5, 0, 1, 8442) V.: It is said only some are chosen for Self realisation and those alone could get it.
(1, 5, 0, 2, 8529) It is rather discouraging.
(1, 5, 1, 3, 8556) B.: All that is meant is, we cannot by our own buddhi, unaided by Gods grace, achieve realisation of Self.
(1, 5, 2, 4, 8663) I added, Bhagavan also says that even that grace does not come arbitrarily, but because one deserves it by ones own efforts either in this or in previous lives.
(1, 5, 3, 5, 8824) V.: Human effort is declared to be useless.
(1, 5, 3, 6, 8868) What incentive can any man then have to better himself?
(1, 5, 4, 7, 8924) I asked, Where is it said you should make no effort or that your effort is useless?
(1, 5, 5, 8, 9008) The visitor thereupon showed the portion in Who am I?
(1, 5, 5, 9, 9062) where it is said, When there is one great Force looking after all the world, why should we bother what we shall do? I pointed out that what is deprecated there is not human effort, but the feeling that I am the doer.
(1, 5, 5, 10, 9279) Bhagavan approved of my explanation, when I asked him if it was not so.
(1, 5, 6, 11, 9351) Af Af Af Af Afttttter er er er ernoon noon noon noon noon
(1, 5, 7, 12, 9409) Bhagavan said he once had a dream that he went to Palni and that he then devoured the Palni God (Lord Subramanya); and that he had at another time a dream that he visited Tiruchendur temple (where also the deity is Lord Subramanya).
(1, 5, 7, 13, 9642) The details of this dream Bhagavan does not remember.
(1, 5, 8, 14, 9696) I remembered that some people once wanted to know if a jivanmukta can have dreams.
(1, 5, 8, 15, 9779) The doubt is natural, because we believe jnanis have no sleep like ordinary men.
(1, 5, 8, 16, 9860) So they may not have dreams.
(1, 5, 8, 17, 9889) I therefore asked Bhagavan about this matter, and he said, If the jnani can have a waking state, what is the difficulty about his having a dream state?
(1, 5, 8, 18, 10041) But of course as his waking state is different from the ordinary mans waking state, so his dream state also will be different from the ordinary mans dream state.
(1, 5, 8, 19, 10203) Whether in waking or in dream he will not slip from his real state which is sometimes called the fourth or turiya state.
(1, 6, 0, 1, 10324) 222224 3 45 Af 4 3 45 Af 4 3 45 Af 4 3 45 Af 4 3 45 Afttttter er er er ernoon noon noon noon noon
(1, 6, 1, 2, 10422) I was reading Vichara Sangraha (Self Enquiry).
(1, 6, 1, 3, 10469) I came across the statement that something in the heart Su Su Fu vjd Lid\, i.e., something is shining or sounding as I I in the heart.
(1, 6, 1, 4, 10604) I have always had doubt what exactly the word sphurana means.
(1, 6, 1, 5, 10666) So I asked Bhagavan and he said, It means [eY or [dY i.e., which shines or illuminates. I asked, Is it not a sound we hear? Bhagavan said, Yes, we may say it is a sound we feel or become aware of.
(1, 6, 1, 6, 10863) He also referred to the dictionary and said, The word means throbbing, springing on the memory, flashing across the mind.
(1, 6, 1, 7, 10985) Thus both sound and light may be implied in the word sphurana.
(1, 6, 1, 8, 11048) Everything has come from light and sound.
(1, 6, 2, 9, 11090) I asked Bhagavan what it is that shines, whether it is the ego or the Self.
(1, 6, 2, 10, 11166) He said it was neither the one, nor the other, but something in between the two, that it is something which is a combination of the I (Self) and the I thought (ego), and that the Self is without even this sphurana.
(1, 6, 3, 11, 11381) Explaining how the Self is mere light and how it is both the word or sound and also that out of which word or sound originally came, Bhagavan said, Man has three bodies, the gross one made of the five elements, the sukshma or subtle one made of manas and prana, and the jiva.
(1, 6, 3, 12, 11657) Similarly even Iswara has three bodies.
(1, 6, 3, 13, 11697) All the manifested universe is His gross body, light and sound are His sukshma body, and the Self His jiva.
(1, 6, 4, 14, 11805) 25 3 45 Af 25 3 45 Af 25 3 45 Af 25 3 45 Af 25 3 45 Afttttter er er er ernoon noon noon noon noon
(1, 6, 5, 15, 11903) One P. Sri Krishniah of Peddapalayam, near Tenali, came to the Asramam on the 22nd and started reading with Bhagavans permission a small work on the life of Dhanurdasa (s[ D\eL p NjWm),
(1, 7, 0, 1, 12089) composed by him in Telugu verse and dedicated to Bhagavan.
(1, 7, 0, 2, 12148) The dedication is both humorous and touching, the poem being regarded as a virgin offered to Bhagavan for marriage, as kanyadana is considered the best gift.
(1, 7, 0, 3, 12306) He seems to have written the dedication first and later composed the work.
(1, 7, 0, 4, 12381) Thus he chose his son in law before he begot his daughter and in the end of his dedication he says, You have already wedded mukti.
(1, 7, 0, 5, 12512) Please take this girl of mine also and treat her kindly and well, correcting her faults and ignoring her weaknesses.
(1, 7, 0, 6, 12629) I cannot ask you to go over to our house, though you have become my son in law, as so many Rajahs and others are always coming to you here for your darshan. He also says, By this marriage of my vaishnava daughter to Bhagavan, advaita and visishtadvaita have become wedded.
(1, 7, 1, 7, 12902) He read the dedication once again on the 25th afternoon and also read out his parting or farewell song of which the following is a free translation: For men like us, various desires often occur.
(1, 7, 1, 8, 13097) Some get fulfilled, others not.
(1, 7, 1, 9, 13129) Here all my desires got their satisfaction.
(1, 7, 1, 10, 13173) One of my desires was to compose a life of Dhanurdasa in verse, another was to come here in company with my friends and relations and to offer the hand of my girl (the poem) to you, a third was to eat to my satisfaction in your company at the above marriage feast, a fourth was to stay here a few days and feast my eyes on a sight of you.
(1, 7, 1, 11, 13512) All these together I got, by virtue of your grace.
(1, 7, 1, 12, 13563) Please give me leave to depart.
(1, 7, 1, 13, 13595) Oh, one of pure life!
(1, 7, 1, 14, 13617) What magic have you stored in this form of yours, that those who have seen it become entranced?
(1, 7, 1, 15, 13713) What power have you instilled in this air, that it is able to ward off all distress?
(1, 7, 1, 16, 13798) What drug have you mixed in the water of this place that it is able to subdue all ailments?
(1, 7, 1, 17, 13890) What powder of enchantment have you spread over these premises that those who come here feel reluctant to depart?
(1, 7, 1, 18, 14004) You alone can know your greatness.
(1, 7, 1, 19, 14039) However long we may remain, the feet will not
(1, 8, 0, 1, 14085) move to depart from here.
(1, 8, 0, 2, 14111) What can I do?
(1, 8, 0, 3, 14126) Please grant me leave to depart, purushottama (best among men).
(1, 8, 1, 4, 14190) 333331 3 45 1 3 45 1 3 45 1 3 45 1 3 45
(1, 8, 2, 5, 14230) One night, a few days ago, after supper, when Bhagavan was resting on his cot in the verandah, east of his hall, something funny took place.
(1, 8, 2, 6, 14371) He was facing south.
(1, 8, 2, 7, 14392) Chadwick was sitting behind Bhagavans back.
(1, 8, 2, 8, 14436) Soon after Bhagavan took his seat and leaned on the cushions, Chadwick from behind stealthily and unnoticed fanned Bhagavan.
(1, 8, 2, 9, 14561) When Bhagavan turned and looked, Chadwick withdrew the fan and remained still.
(1, 8, 2, 10, 14640) When Bhagavan turned his face south, Chadwick resumed fanning again.
(1, 8, 2, 11, 14709) Bhagavan turned round and Chadwick stopped.
(1, 8, 2, 12, 14753) Bhagavan was left wondering how he got the breeze.
(1, 8, 2, 13, 14804) Chadwick then laughed out and Bhagavan joined in the laughter.
(1, 8, 2, 14, 14867) This shows how even with such an eminent Master a devotee can play and both can enjoy the joke like children.
(1, 8, 3, 15, 14977) Af Af Af Af Afttttter er er er ernoon noon noon noon noon
(1, 8, 4, 16, 15035) It seems a visitor, the Raja of Sivaghar in U.P., told Bhagavan that he had surrendered himself to Bhagavan and Bhagavan should give him jnana.
(1, 8, 4, 17, 15179) Bhagavan referred to an article on Nama Devs insistence on the importance of the Lords name, in the September 1937 issue of Vision, where it is pointed out that only when the I, the ego, is surrendered the significance of the Lords name will be realized.
(1, 8, 4, 18, 15434) When I entered the hall the story of how Ashtavakra Gita came to be taught was being recounted in English, for the benefit of the above Raja and other visitors.
(1, 8, 4, 19, 15595) After the story was read out, Bhagavan said, Because Brahma jnana is not something external, which is somewhere far away where you can go and get it, you cannot say that it will take so long or so short a time to attain it.
(1, 8, 4, 20, 15819) It is always with you.
(1, 8, 4, 21, 15842) You are That!
(1, 8, 4, 22, 15856) The story of Ashtavakra Gita is intended to teach that for getting Brahma jnana all that is necessary is to
(1, 9, 0, 1, 15964) surrender yourself completely to the guru, to surrender your notion of I and mine.
(1, 9, 0, 2, 16047) If these are surrendered, what remains is the Reality.
(1, 9, 0, 3, 16102) Then, it becomes impossible to state what further time it would take to attain Brahma jnana.
(1, 9, 0, 4, 16195) It would be wrong to state that it takes as much time as a man would require to put his other foot into the second stirrup after having placed one foot in the first stirrup.
(1, 9, 0, 5, 16369) The moment when ego is completely surrendered, the Self shines.
(1, 9, 1, 6, 16433) Proceeding, Bhagavan quoted the last two lines of the following stanza from Yoga Vasishta: S]m TsL Ro O]Y LVe Lo.
(1, 9, 1, 7, 16547) S]m T}d LiPo O]Y LV UYo; S] ULeLWeLs O]i UV ]m TWU YmT ]uUXo VRu\. which state that unless the cloud of the I or ego sense which covers the moon of the Divine consciousness (chidakasa) is removed, the lily of the heart which knows nothing of the sense of I (ahankara) will not open out in full bloom.
(1, 9, 2, 8, 16848) Bhagavan also added, We have to contend against age long samskaras.
(1, 9, 2, 9, 16916) They will all go.
(1, 9, 2, 10, 16934) Only, they go comparatively soon in the case of those who have already made sadhana in the past, and late in the case of the others. In this connection I asked, Do these samskaras go gradually or will they suddenly disappear one day?
(1, 9, 2, 11, 17168) I ask this, because though I have remained fairly long here I do not perceive any gradual change in me. Bhagavan asked, When the sun rises, does the darkness go gradually or all at once?
(1, 9, 3, 12, 17355) Another visitor asked, How to conquer passions? Bhagavan said, If the passions are something external to us we can take arms and ammunition and conquer them.
(1, 9, 3, 13, 17513) They all come from within us.
(1, 9, 3, 14, 17543) If, by looking into the source whence they issue, we see that they dont come out of us, we shall conquer them.
(1, 9, 3, 15, 17654) It is the world and the objects in it that arouse our passions.
(1, 9, 3, 16, 17718) But the world and these objects are only created by our mind.
(1, 9, 3, 17, 17780) They dont exist during our sleep.
(1, 10, 0, 1, 17814) After all this talk Bhagavan drank a little water from his kamandalam and turning to his attendant enquired whether he had already drunk some water (i.e., after he returned to the hall about 3 30 P.M.).
(1, 10, 0, 2, 18017) The attendant said yes, and thereupon Bhagavan said he had forgotten it, and to make sure he drank some again.
(1, 10, 0, 3, 18128) He further added, almost in an unguarded moment, as he rarely gives expression to such experiences of his, that sometimes he does not even know whether it is morning, mid day or evening and has to look at the clock and try to remember before he knows what time of day it is.
(1, 10, 0, 4, 18403) On one occasion, he has told me that he scratched his skin, where there was eczema once, as we scratch during sleep.
(1, 10, 0, 5, 18520) And once when I was concerned over some physical pain of his, he told me he feels that pain LdLiPtTp, i.e., it was a passing and faint experience like that in a dream.
(1, 10, 0, 6, 18688) These are clues to the sort of life Bhagavan leads in our midst, seeming to act and move and feel as we do, but really living in a world of his own where the things we experience dont exist.
(1, 10, 1, 7, 18879) 5 6 45 Af 5 6 45 Af 5 6 45 Af 5 6 45 Af 5 6 45 Afttttter er er er ernoon noon noon noon noon
(1, 10, 2, 8, 18972) Myself, Harindranath Chattopadhyaya, G.V. Subbaramayya and T. P. Ramachandra Aiyar were sitting in the front row just opposite Bhagavan in the hall and G.V.S. said to H.C. I recently came across a typed copy of some of your verses made at Aurobindo Ashram, with Sri Aurobindos notes on the margin highly commending some verses. Thereupon H.C. told Bhagavan, I stayed at Aurobindos Ashram for two years and I then made about 4000 sonnets and a poem of 50,000 lines plus other poetry. Apparently the fact that H.C. had been at Aurobindos Ashram before for two years was news to Bhagavan, though it was not to some of us.
(1, 10, 2, 9, 19591) This is the third visit of H.C. to Bhagavan.
(1, 10, 2, 10, 19636) H.C. then gave us a recitation of two of his earliest poems and one out of those made at Pondicherry.
(1, 10, 2, 11, 19738) They are given below.
(1, 10, 2, 12, 19760) Bhagavan enjoyed the recitation.
(1, 11, 0, 1, 19793) THE EARTHEN GOBLET
(1, 11, 1, 2, 19812) (A conversation between the poet and the goblet)
(1, 11, 2, 3, 19861) O silent goblet!
(1, 11, 2, 4, 19878) red from head to heel, How did you feel When you were being twirled Upon the Potters wheel Before the Potter gave you to the world? I felt a conscious impulse in my clay To break away From the great Potters hand That burned so warm.
(1, 11, 2, 5, 20111) I felt a vast Feeling of sorrow to be cast Into my present form.
(1, 11, 2, 6, 20176) Before that fatal hour That saw me captive on the Potters wheel And cast into this crimson goblet sleep, I used to feel The fragrant friendship of a little flower Whose root was in my bosom buried deep.
(1, 11, 2, 7, 20379) The Potter has drawn out the living breath of me, And given me a form which is the death of me; My past unshapely natural state was best, With just one flower flaming through my breast.
(1, 11, 3, 8, 20565) PITCHERS OF CLAY
(1, 11, 4, 9, 20582) Outside the Potters shop upon the way In patient rows we stand, pitchers of clay  Under a copper clouded sky of gold Expecting every moment to be sold.
(1, 11, 5, 10, 20734) Although we have no language, yet we feel A bitterness towards the Potters wheel
(1, 12, 0, 1, 20815) Which moulded us, what though without a flaw, To shape, which is against our beings law.
(1, 12, 1, 2, 20904) Pitchers are beautiful and yet, indeed, Even from beauty we would all be freed And, slipping into Earth, secure escape From the enchanted tyranny of shape.
(1, 12, 2, 3, 21060) Some of us pitchers, tired of being, drop And break to pieces in the Potters shop.
(1, 12, 2, 4, 21143) Pathetic things!
(1, 12, 2, 5, 21160) What does the Potter care For the pale weariness of Earthenware?
(1, 12, 3, 6, 21225) SHAPER SHAPED
(1, 12, 4, 7, 21239) In days gone by I used to be A potter who would feel His fingers mould the yielding clay To patterns on his wheel; But now, through wisdom lately won, That pride has died away, I have ceased to be the potter And have learned to be the clay.
(1, 12, 5, 8, 21480) In other days I used to be A poet through whose pen Innumerable songs would come To win the hearts of men; But now, through new got knowledge Which I hadnt had so long, I have ceased to be the poet And have learned to be the song.
(1, 12, 6, 9, 21711) I was a fashioner of swords, In days that now are gone, Which on a hundred battle fields Glittered and gleamed and shone;
(1, 13, 0, 1, 21833) But now that I am brimming with The silence of the Lord I have ceased to be a sword maker And learned to be the sword.
(1, 13, 1, 2, 21952) In by gone days I used to be A dreamer who would hurl On every side an insolence Of emerald and pearl.
(1, 13, 1, 3, 22055) But now that I am kneeling At the feet of the Supreme I have ceased to be the dreamer And have learned to be the dream.
(1, 13, 2, 4, 22175) After this I asked H.C. to recite before Bhagavan (or rather act as on the stage) a piece from a play of his in which a dock labourer groaning under his work bursts out into a complaint.
(1, 13, 2, 5, 22362) H.C. did so and all saw how moving a good recitation can be.
(1, 13, 2, 6, 22423) After a while H.C. asked Bhagavan, How is it, Bhagavan, we sometimes feel choked with tears in Bhagavans Presence? Bhagavan smiled and kept quiet.
(1, 13, 2, 7, 22570) I said, It is a good thing if ones tears gush forth like that and even of Bhagavan it is recorded that when he used to go and stand before the image in the temple at Madura, before he came here, tears used to flow involuntarily out of his eyes, not as the result of any joy or pain, but purely out of bhakti. Bhagavan was thereupon kind enough to add, Even after coming here such a thing has happened.
(1, 13, 2, 8, 22972) Even on reading or hearing touching passages from books such a thing has happened.
(1, 13, 2, 9, 23055) Apparently a stock of emotional tears is latent in so many of us, so that at any opportune moment, or on the slightest provocation, they well out without any control on our part. Then Bhagavan narrated, very dramatically as is usual with him, an incident which occurred when he was about 22 and living in the Virupakshi Cave.
(1, 13, 2, 10, 23381) It seems he was sitting on a rock near the cave and a boy of about 8 or 10 years came there,
(1, 14, 0, 1, 23474) looked at Bhagavan and, not being able to bear the sight of such a young and bright person taking to such a hard life of penance, was so moved to compassion that he started to sob and sobbed violently for some time.
(1, 14, 0, 2, 23690) Bhagavan said, Who could say what was the reason for his sobbing and why tears flowed out of him merely at his seeing me? Bhagavan continued in a reminiscent mood later in the day and added that another boy, also about 8 or 10 years old, met Bhagavan another day in his Virupakshi cave days and took such pity on Bhagavan that the following conversation took place between them.
(1, 14, 0, 3, 24069) Bhagavan was sitting on a rock near the cave, all alone, and the boy came and met him there.
(1, 14, 1, 4, 24162) Boy: Why are you here, all alone, like this?
(1, 14, 2, 5, 24207) Bhagavan: I had some trouble at home and so have come away like this.
(1, 14, 3, 6, 24277) Boy: Then how about your food?
(1, 14, 4, 7, 24308) Bhagavan: I eat if anybody gives me anything to eat.
(1, 14, 5, 8, 24361) Boy: I have a good master.
(1, 14, 5, 9, 24388) I shall take you to him.
(1, 14, 5, 10, 24413) First, you may have to volunteer your services free.
(1, 14, 5, 11, 24466) If he approves of your work, he will give you three pies a day and gradually he will increase it to six pies, and so on.
(1, 14, 6, 12, 24587) Bhagavan: Yes, please do so.
(1, 14, 7, 13, 24616) Bhagavan added, There was no doubt that the boy was very much concerned over what he considered my sad plight and that he was moved by great and genuine pity.

(1, 16, 3, 11, 28221) Mr. Subba Rao of Bezwada asked Bhagavan, What is the difference between imagination and vision?
(1, 16, 4, 12, 28317) Bhagavan: One is voluntary and the other is not.
(1, 16, 4, 13, 28366) But in the ultimate analysis, though not in the immediate present, even vision must have had its origin in the voluntary sphere.
(1, 16, 5, 14, 28495) Subba Rao: As dreams have their origin there?
(1, 16, 6, 15, 28541) B: Yes.
(1, 16, 7, 16, 28549) Another Visitor: It is said that our waking life is also a dream, similar to our dream during sleep.
(1, 16, 7, 17, 28650) But in our dreams
(1, 17, 0, 1, 28668) we make no conscious effort to get rid of the dream and to awake, but the dream itself comes to an end without any effort on our part and we become awake.
(1, 17, 0, 2, 28823) Similarly why should not the waking state, which is in reality only another sort of dream, come to an end of its own accord, and without any effort on our part, and land us in jnana or real awakening?
(1, 17, 1, 3, 29024) B: Your thinking that you have to make an effort to get rid of this dream of the waking state and your making efforts to attain jnana or real awakening are all parts of the dream.
(1, 17, 1, 4, 29204) When you attain jnana you will see there was neither the dream during sleep, nor the waking state, but only yourself and your real state.
(1, 17, 2, 5, 29342) I pressed Bhagavan, But what is the answer to the question?
(1, 17, 2, 6, 29402) Why should not the waking state also pass like our dreams without any effort on our part and land us in jnana, as a dream passes off and leaves us awake?
(1, 17, 3, 7, 29556) B: Who can say that the dream passed off of its own accord?
(1, 17, 3, 8, 29616) If the dream came on, as is generally supposed, as the result of our past thoughts or karma, probably the same karma also decides how long it should last and how after that time it should cease.
(1, 17, 4, 9, 29811) I was still unsatisfied and, as the result of further talk with Bhagavan, I feel that the waking state, though a sort of dream, is clearly distinct from the dream during sleep in this, namely that during dream it never occurs to us that it is a dream, whereas in the waking state we are able to argue and understand from books and gurus and from some phenomena that it may be only dream after all.
(1, 17, 4, 10, 30209) Because of this, it may be our duty to make an effort to wake into jnana.
(1, 17, 4, 11, 30283) Bhagavan says that we dont deem a dream, a dream till we wake up, that the dream looks quite real while it lasts; and that similarly this waking state will not appear a dream till we wake up into jnana.
(1, 17, 4, 12, 30486) Still, it seems to me that, because of the above difference between the dream and the waking states, our effort is called for.

(1, 18, 1, 2, 30653) Three or four days ago Mr. Desai, Retired Sub Judge, asked Bhagavan (with reference to what is said in Ramana Gita), How to direct the prana or life current into the sushumna nadi, so that as stated in Ramana Gita we could achieve the severance of the chit jada granthi? Bhagavan said, By enquiring Who am I?
(1, 18, 2, 3, 30962) The yogi may be definitely aiming at rousing the kundalini and sending it up the sushumna.
(1, 18, 2, 4, 31053) The jnani may not be having this as his object.
(1, 18, 2, 5, 31101) But both achieve the same result, that of sending the Life force up the sushumna and severing the chit jada granthi.
(1, 18, 2, 6, 31218) Kundalini is only another name for atma or Self or sakti.
(1, 18, 2, 7, 31276) We talk of it as being inside the body, because we conceive ourselves as limited by this body.
(1, 18, 2, 8, 31371) But it is in reality both inside and outside, being no other than Self or the sakti of Self.
(1, 18, 3, 9, 31464) Desai: How to churn up the nadis, so that the kundalini may go up the sushumna?
(1, 18, 4, 10, 31544) Bhagavan: Though the yogi may have his methods of breath control, pranayama, mudras, etc., for this object, the jnanis method is only that of enquiry.
(1, 18, 4, 11, 31695) When by this method the mind is merged in the Self, the Self, its sakti or kundalini, rises automatically.
(1, 18, 5, 12, 31802) The next day a visitor asked Bhagavan, with reference to the words dhimahi in the gayatri, What is the idea meant?
(1, 18, 5, 13, 31917) I am not able rightly to grasp it.
(1, 18, 6, 14, 31952) B: The words only mean fixing the aham in the Self, though literally they mean, We meditate.
(1, 18, 7, 15, 32045) Visitor: I am not able to form a conception of the Tat or the Self.
(1, 18, 7, 16, 32113) Then, how am I to fix the aham in the Tat.
(1, 18, 8, 17, 32156) B: Why should you bother to conceive the Tat which you dont know?
(1, 18, 8, 18, 32222) Try to find out the I that you know, what it is and whence it arises.
(1, 18, 8, 19, 32292) That is enough.

(1, 19, 1, 2, 32406) A visitor asked, What should one, who is an absolute beginner, do in this (i.e., spiritual) line?
(1, 19, 2, 3, 32504) Bhagavan: The very fact that you put this question shows you know what to do.
(1, 19, 2, 4, 32582) It is because you feel the want of peace, that you are anxious to take some steps to secure peace.
(1, 19, 2, 5, 32681) Because I have a little pain in my foot, I am applying this ointment.
(1, 19, 3, 6, 32751) Visitor: What is the method to be adopted for securing peace?
(1, 19, 4, 7, 32813) B: The conception that there is a goal and a path to it, is wrong.
(1, 19, 4, 8, 32880) We are the goal or peace always.
(1, 19, 4, 9, 32913) To get rid of the notion that we are not peace is all that is required.
(1, 19, 5, 10, 32985) V: All books say that the guidance of a Guru is necessary.
(1, 19, 6, 11, 33044) B: The Guru will say only what I am saying now.
(1, 19, 6, 12, 33092) He will not give you anything you have not already.

(1, 19, 6, 15, 33263) What comes will also go.
(1, 19, 6, 16, 33288) What always is will alone remain.
(1, 19, 6, 17, 33322) The Guru cannot give you anything new, which you have not already.
(1, 19, 6, 18, 33389) Removal of the notion that we have not realised the Self is all that is required.
(1, 19, 6, 19, 33471) We are always the Self.
(1, 19, 6, 20, 33495) Only, we dont realise it.
(1, 19, 7, 21, 33521) The Asramam compounder asked some questions about his experiences during meditation.
(1, 19, 7, 22, 33606) Bhagavan explained that the Self is the one reality that always exists and it is by its light all other things are seen.
(1, 19, 7, 23, 33727) We forget it and concentrate on the appearances.
(1, 19, 7, 24, 33776) The light in the hall burns, both when persons are present there and when they are absent, both when persons are enacting something as in a theatre and when nothing is being enacted.
(1, 19, 7, 25, 33959) It is the light which enabled us to see the hall, the persons and the acting.
(1, 19, 7, 26, 34037) We are so engrossed with the objects or appearances revealed by the light that we pay no attention to the light.
(1, 19, 7, 27, 34150) In the waking state or dream state, in which things appear, and in the sleep state, in which we see nothing, there is always
(1, 20, 0, 1, 34275) the light of consciousness or Self, like the hall lamp always burning.
(1, 20, 0, 2, 34346) The thing to do is to concentrate on the seer and not on the seen, not on the objects, but on the Light which reveals them.

(1, 20, 2, 4, 34568) A group of Bengalis have come.
(1, 20, 2, 5, 34599) One of them has recently lost a child.
(1, 20, 2, 6, 34638) He put the question to Bhagavan, Why did that child die so young?
(1, 20, 2, 7, 34704) Is it his karma or our karma that we should have this grief?
(1, 20, 3, 8, 34765) Bhagavan: The prarabdha which the child had to work out in this life was over and so it passed away.
(1, 20, 3, 9, 34866) So we may call it the childs karma.
(1, 20, 3, 10, 34902) So far as you are concerned, it is open to you not to grieve over it, but to remain calm and unaffected by it, being convinced that the child was not yours but always only Gods, that God gave and God took away.
(1, 20, 3, 11, 35113) And in this connection Bhagavan took out the Yoga Vasishta in English to refer to the story of Punya and Pavana.
(1, 20, 3, 12, 35226) Strange to say, when he casually opened the book, it actually opened at the story he had in mind.
(1, 20, 3, 13, 35324) And from the book he asked me to read out the portion where Punya advises his brother Pavana not to grieve foolishly over the death of their parents, pointing out that Pavana had had innumerable births in the past, in each one of which he had a number of relations and that exactly as he is not mourning for the death of all those relations now, he should not now mourn for the death of their father either.
(1, 20, 4, 14, 35732) The visitor asked, When a person dies while yet a child and another lives long, which of them is the greater sinner?
(1, 20, 5, 15, 35849) B: I cannot say.
(1, 20, 6, 16, 35866) I told the visitor that the data he had given could not by themselves enable anyone to judge which was the greater sinner.
(1, 20, 7, 17, 35989) Visitor: If a person lives long, he has greater chances of perfecting steps to reach realisation.
(1, 21, 0, 1, 36087) B: The person dying young may soon be reborn and have in that life better chances of striving towards realisation than the other person living long in this life.
(1, 21, 1, 2, 36249) A visitor asked, When it is said that we must renounce all activities, is it meant that we should reduce our activities as much as possible?
(1, 21, 2, 3, 36390) B: By giving up activities is meant giving up attachment to activities or the fruits thereof, giving up the notion I am the doer.
(1, 21, 2, 4, 36520) The activities for going through which this body has come, will have to be gone through.
(1, 21, 2, 5, 36609) There is no question of giving up such activities, whatever one may or may not like.

(1, 23, 1, 2, 39991) It was reported to Bhagavan that somehow under cover of the leaves, two mangoes had escaped the notice of the monkeys and had grown to quite a big size and that they were discovered today, and that one was taken away by the monkeys and the other left behind.

(1, 23, 1, 4, 40618) Neither the head of the mutt nor the disciples knew anything about this man.

(1, 23, 1, 7, 40871) Only a jnani can recognise a jnani.
(1, 23, 1, 8, 40907) One who indulges in kriya, charya, or yoga can be recognised by these activities which he goes through.

(1, 24, 3, 5, 42211) Mr. K. Mahatani asked in continuation of the above, If we want to succeed in any enterprise in the world, we must give our whole mind and heart to it.

(1, 26, 3, 5, 45936) Mr. G.V. Subbaramayya arrived.

(1, 26, 3, 7, 46068) A few days ago Mr. P.C. Desai brought to Bhagavans notice a Gujarati book on Dakshinamurti Stotra, written by himself.
(1, 26, 3, 8, 46187) And in connection with it Bhagavan asked me to make for Mr. P.C. Desais benefit an English translation of Bhagavans Tamil preface to the Stotra.
(1, 26, 3, 9, 46332) This led Smt.
(1, 26, 3, 10, 46346) Nagamma to make a Telugu translation.
(1, 26, 3, 11, 46384) Reading and explaining the above Telugu translation, Bhagavan told Mr. G.V. Subbaramayya practically all that he had told Mr. P.C. Desai before.
(1, 26, 3, 12, 46529) The gist of it is this: Dakshinamurti, i.e., the great Siva himself could not express the truth of the one Reality except by silence.
(1, 26, 3, 13, 46663) But that silence could not be understood except by the very advanced.
(1, 26, 3, 14, 46733) The others have to be told.
(1, 26, 3, 15, 46761) And yet how is one to say in words that which God himself could not express?
(1, 26, 3, 16, 46838) Sankara therefore advises the method of praising Dakshinamurti and with that as the ostensible object really seeks to explain that all is Brahman.
(1, 26, 3, 17, 46985) In the first four stanzas he explains the nature of the world, since what prevents our knowing the reality is the world and if its (i.e., worlds) nature is understood, the obstacle in the way of realising truth will be removed.
(1, 26, 3, 18, 47213) In the next four stanzas he explains the nature of the jiva.
(1, 26, 3, 19, 47274) Then he explains the connection between the two and teaches that all is Self.
(1, 26, 3, 20, 47352) Trying to explain the scheme and gist of Sankaras Dakshinamurti Stotra, I wrote the above brief preface.

(1, 27, 1, 2, 47550) A visitor from the Punjab asked Bhagavan, When I meditate I feel a certain bliss at times.
(1, 27, 1, 3, 47641) On such occasions, should I ask myself Who is it that experiences this bliss?
(1, 27, 2, 4, 47719) Bhagavan: If it is the real bliss of the Self that is experienced, i.e., if the mind has merged really in the Self, such a doubt will not arise at all.
(1, 27, 2, 5, 47871) The question itself shows real bliss was not reached.
(1, 27, 3, 6, 47925) All doubts will cease only when the doubter and his source have been found.
(1, 27, 3, 7, 48001) There is no use removing doubts.
(1, 27, 3, 8, 48034) If we clear one doubt, another doubt will arise and there will be no end of doubts.
(1, 27, 3, 9, 48118) But if the doubter is found to be really non existent, by seeking for the source of the doubter, then all doubts will cease.
(1, 27, 4, 10, 48243) Visitor: Sometimes I hear internal sounds.
(1, 27, 4, 11, 48286) What should I do when such things happen?
(1, 27, 5, 12, 48328) Bhagavan: Whatever may happen, keep up the enquiry into the Self, asking Who hears these sounds? till the reality is reached.

(1, 28, 4, 12, 50863) A barrister from Bombay asked Bhagavan, I have read the works of Bhagavan and others and, though I can understand them intellectually, I have not been able to realise anything in experience.
(1, 28, 4, 13, 51054) I have tried Bhagavans method for about six years and yet I have not made any progress.
(1, 28, 4, 14, 51142) When I meditate, other thoughts come.
(1, 28, 4, 15, 51180) For people like me, living in cities and doing our work and coming here only occasionally, what sadhana
(1, 29, 0, 1, 51284) would Bhagavan advise so that we may succeed better than I have so far been able to do?
(1, 29, 1, 2, 51372) Bhagavan: Your real nature is always there, your meditation, etc., come only temporarily.
(1, 29, 1, 3, 51462) Reality being your Self, there is nothing for you to realise.
(1, 29, 1, 4, 51524) All that is required is that you should give up regarding the unreal as real, which is what all are doing.
(1, 29, 1, 5, 51631) The object of all meditation, dhyana or japa is only that, to give up all thoughts regarding the not self, to give up many thoughts and to keep to the one thought.
(1, 29, 2, 6, 51795) As for sadhana, there are many methods.
(1, 29, 2, 7, 51835) You may do vichara, asking yourself Who am I? or, if that does not appeal to you, you may do dhyana I am Brahman or otherwise, or you may concentrate on a mantra or name in japa.
(1, 29, 2, 8, 52014) The object is to make the mind one pointed, to concentrate it on one thought and thus exclude our many thoughts, and if we do this, eventually even the one thought will go and the mind will get extinguished in its source.
(1, 29, 3, 9, 52236) Visitor: In actual practice I find I am not able to succeed in my efforts.
(1, 29, 3, 10, 52311) Unless Bhagavans grace descends on me I cannot succeed.
(1, 29, 4, 11, 52367) Bhagavan: Gurus grace is always there.
(1, 29, 4, 12, 52406) You imagine it is something, somewhere high up in the sky, far away, and has to descend.
(1, 29, 4, 13, 52495) It is really inside you, in your heart, and the moment (by any of the methods) you effect subsidence or merger of the mind into its source, the grace rushes forth, spouting as from a spring, from within you.
(1, 29, 5, 14, 52703) Another visitor asked, What is the reality of this world?
(1, 29, 6, 15, 52761) Bhagavan: If you know your reality first, you will be able to know the reality of the world.
(1, 29, 6, 16, 52854) It is a strange thing that most people do not care to know about their own reality, but are very anxious to know about the reality of the world.
(1, 30, 0, 1, 52999) You realise your own Self first and then see if the world exists independently of you and is able to come and assert before you its reality or existence.
(1, 30, 1, 2, 53153) Another visitor asked, Why is there so much pain even for the innocent, such as children for instance?

(1, 30, 2, 6, 53419) All these differences, the pains and miseries of the innocent, as you say, do they exist independently of you?

(1, 33, 3, 7, 59646) Dilip Kumar Roy, singer and author, who is on a visit here from Sri Aurobindo Ashram, asked Bhagavan, According to the Maha Yoga you say that the sages have not said anything to contradict each other.
(1, 33, 3, 8, 59847) Yet, we find one advocating bhakti, another jnana, etc., leading thus to all sorts of quarrels.
(1, 33, 4, 9, 59943) Bhagavan: There is really nothing contradictory in such teachings.
(1, 33, 4, 10, 60010) When for instance a follower of bhakti marga declares that bhakti is the best, he really means by the word bhakti what the jnana marga man calls jnana.
(1, 33, 4, 11, 60162) There is no difference in the state or its description by attributes or transcendence of attributes.
(1, 33, 4, 12, 60263) Only different thinkers have used different words.
(1, 33, 4, 13, 60314) All these different margas, or paths or sadhanas lead to the same goal.
(1, 33, 4, 14, 60386) What is once a means becomes itself the goal.
(1, 33, 4, 15, 60432) When that happens dhyana, bhakti or jnana, which was at one time a conscious and painful effort, becomes the normal and natural state, spontaneously and without effort.

(1, 37, 6, 17, 67450) When (on 2 11 45) Mr. Roy asked Bhagavan the best way of killing the ego, Bhagavan said, To ask the mind to kill the mind is like making the thief the policeman.
(1, 37, 6, 18, 67612) He will go with you and pretend to catch the thief, but nothing will be gained.
(1, 37, 6, 19, 67692) So you must turn inward and see where the mind rises from and then it will cease to exist. In reference to this answer, Mr. Thambi Thorai of Jaffna (who has been living in Palakothu for over a year) asked me, whether asking the mind to turn inward and seek its source is not also employing the mind.
(1, 37, 6, 20, 67992) So, I put this doubt before
(1, 38, 0, 1, 68020) Bhagavan and Bhagavan said, Of course we are employing the mind.
(1, 38, 0, 2, 68085) It is well known and admitted that only with the help of the mind the mind has to be killed.
(1, 38, 0, 3, 68178) But instead of setting about saying there is a mind, and I want to kill it, you begin to seek the source of the mind, and you find the mind does not exist at all.
(1, 38, 0, 4, 68341) The mind, turned outwards, results in thoughts and objects.
(1, 38, 0, 5, 68401) Turned inwards, it becomes itself the Self.
(1, 38, 0, 6, 68445) Such a mind is sometimes called arupa manas or suddha manas.

(1, 41, 1, 2, 73473) The Swami was asking Bhagavan about the characteristics of a jnani.
(1, 41, 1, 3, 73541) Bhagavan said they are all described in books, such as the Bhagavad Gita, but that we must bear in mind that the jnanis state being one which transcends the mind cannot be described with the help merely of the mind and that all description therefore must be defective.
(1, 41, 1, 4, 73810) Only silence can correctly describe their state or characteristics.
(1, 41, 1, 5, 73878) But silence is more effective than speech.
(1, 41, 1, 6, 73921) From silence came thought, from thought the ego and from the ego speech.
(1, 41, 1, 7, 73994) So if speech is effective, how much more effective must its original source be?
(1, 41, 1, 8, 74074) In this connection Bhagavan related the following story: Tattvaraya composed a bharani (a kind of poetic composition in Tamil) in honour of his Guru Swarupananda and convened an assembly of learned pandits to hear the work and assess its value.
(1, 41, 1, 9, 74319) The pandits raised the objection that a bharani was only composed in honour of great heroes capable of killing a thousand elephants, and that it was not in order to compose such a work in honour of an ascetic.
(1, 41, 1, 10, 74529) Thereupon the author said, Let us all go to my Guru and we shall have this matter settled there.
(1, 41, 1, 11, 74626) They went to the Guru and, after all had taken their seats, the author told his Guru the purpose of their coming there.
(1, 41, 1, 12, 74746) The Guru sat silent and all the others also remained in mauna.
(1, 41, 1, 13, 74809) The whole day passed, night came, and some more days and nights, and yet all sat there silently, no thought at all occurring to any of them and nobody thinking or asking why they had come there.
(1, 41, 1, 14, 75004) After three or four days like this, the Guru moved his mind a bit, and thereupon the assembly regained their thought activity.
(1, 41, 1, 15, 75131) They then declared, Conquering a thousand elephants is nothing beside this Gurus power to conquer the rutting elephants of all our egos put together.
(1, 41, 1, 16, 75281) So certainly he deserves the bharani in his honour!

(1, 43, 1, 3, 77269) Dr. Srinivasa Rao asked Bhagavan, What is the meaning of eLUp eY, i.e., being in sleepless sleep?
(1, 43, 2, 4, 77367) Bhagavan: It is the jnanis state.
(1, 43, 2, 5, 77401) In sleep our ego is submerged and the sense organs are not active.
(1, 43, 2, 6, 77468) The jnanis ego has been killed and he does not indulge in any sense activities of his own accord or with the notion that he is the doer.
(1, 43, 2, 7, 77605) So he is in sleep.
(1, 43, 2, 8, 77624) At the same time he is not unconscious as in sleep, but fully awake in the Self; so his state is sleepless.
(1, 43, 2, 9, 77732) This sleepless sleep, wakeful sleep, or whatever it may be called, is the turiya state of the Self, on which as the screen, all the three avasthas, the waking, dream and sleep, pass, leaving the screen unaffected. Bhagavan said that instead of holding on to that which exists, we are looking for that which does not.
(1, 43, 2, 10, 78049) We bother about the past and the future, not realising the truth of the present.

(1, 44, 5, 13, 79737) I asked Bhagavan, It seems this morning Rishikesananda quoted some text which says wherever the mind goes, that is samadhi.

(1, 44, 6, 18, 79979) Whatever they may be doing, there is no break in their samadhi state.
(1, 44, 6, 19, 80049) Their bodies may be engaged in whatever activities they were intended by prarabdha to go through.
(1, 44, 6, 20, 80147) But they are always in the Self.

(1, 44, 6, 22, 80262) The Bhagavad Gita says, The wise man will think the senses move among the sense objects and be unattached to the activities of the sense organs. I would go farther and say that the jnani does not think even that.
(1, 44, 6, 23, 80475) He is the Self and sees nothing apart from himself.
(1, 44, 6, 24, 80527) What the Bhagavad Gita says in the
(1, 45, 0, 1, 80562) above passage is for the abhyasi or the practiser.
(1, 45, 0, 2, 80613) There is no harm in engaging in whatever activities naturally come to one.
(1, 45, 0, 3, 80688) The hindrance or bondage is in imagining that we are the doers and attaching ourselves to the fruits of such activities.
(1, 45, 1, 4, 80809) In this connection Bhagavan also said, A man says I came from Madras.

(1, 45, 1, 6, 80911) The jutka or some other vehicle brought him from his house to the railway station, the train brought him to Tiruvannamalai railway station, and from there some other cart brought him here.
(1, 45, 1, 7, 81100) But he says I came.
(1, 45, 1, 8, 81120) This is how we identify ourselves with the acts of the body and the senses. Bhagavan also quoted from the Vedanta Chudamani to the effect that the activities of the jnani are all samadhi, i.e.

(1, 46, 4, 10, 83269) Bhagavan explained how it is said in books that the highest possible happiness, which a human being can attain or which the ten grades of beings higher than man, ending with gods like Brahma can attain, is like foam in the deluging flood of the bliss of the Self.
(1, 46, 5, 11, 83533) Imagine a man in robust health; of vigorous adult age, endowed with unsurpassed wealth and power, with intellect and all other resources, and married to a fair and faithful wife, and conceive of his happiness.
(1, 46, 6, 12, 83743) Each higher grade of being above man is capable of a hundred fold greater happiness than that of the grade below.
(1, 46, 6, 13, 83857) But the highest happiness of all the eleven grades of being is only the foam in the flooding ocean of divine bliss.
(1, 46, 7, 14, 83973) In this connection Bhagavan narrated the following story:
(1, 46, 8, 15, 84031) A king was passing through a forest in all his pomp and pageantry, with his army and retinue behind him.
(1, 46, 8, 16, 84136) He came across a man with not even a cod piece on him, lying on the
(1, 47, 0, 1, 84204) ground, with one leg cocked over the other and laughing away, apparently supremely happy and contented with himself and all the world.
(1, 47, 0, 2, 84339) The king was struck with the mans happy state and sent for him.
(1, 47, 0, 3, 84403) But when the kings men approached the nude ascetic and delivered the kings message, he took absolutely no notice and continued in his ascetic bliss.
(1, 47, 0, 4, 84552) On being told of this, the king himself went to the man and even then the man took no notice.
(1, 47, 0, 5, 84646) Thereupon it struck the king that this must be no common man, and said: Swami, you are evidently supremely happy.
(1, 47, 0, 6, 84760) May we know what is the secret of such happiness and from which Guru you learnt it? Thereupon the ascetic told the king: I have had twenty four Gurus.
(1, 47, 0, 7, 84911) Everything, this body, the earth, the birds, some instruments, some persons all have taught me. All the things in the world may be classed as either good or bad.
(1, 47, 0, 8, 85073) The good taught him what he must seek.
(1, 47, 0, 9, 85112) Similarly, the bad taught him what he must avoid.

(1, 47, 1, 15, 85533) When I was in Virupakshi Cave, one day I suddenly found one tooth gave sharp pain when the cold rice came in contact with it and I could eat no more.
(1, 47, 1, 16, 85683) I stopped eating and thought I would have to die of starvation.
(1, 47, 1, 17, 85747) Vasudeva Sastri was then living with me.

(1, 48, 1, 4, 86623) When the Periyar dam was constructed and the water first allowed to pass in the canals, the water came on in a flood and there was plenty of fish in the water.
(1, 48, 1, 5, 86783) The fishermen used to divert the water by means of a side channel and let it into a pond into which they had thrown a number of bundles of tobacco stems, i.e., the stumps after the leaves had been utilised for making cigars.
(1, 48, 1, 6, 87008) The moment the fish got into the pond they became unconscious or dead on account of the poison of the tobacco and began to float.
(1, 48, 1, 7, 87138) And the fishermen got heaps of fish in this way.

(1, 50, 0, 1, 89881) of penances to become what one already is.

(1, 50, 1, 3, 90057) Later Bhagavan said, The spark of jnana will easily consume all creation as if it were a mountain heap of cotton.

(1, 50, 1, 5, 90479) You say you offer your body, soul and all possessions to God.
(1, 50, 1, 6, 90541) Were they yours that you could offer them?

(1, 50, 1, 10, 90949) Bhakti is jnana mata or mother of jnana.
(1, 50, 2, 11, 90990) Talking of the innumerable ways of different seekers after God, Bhagavan said, Each should be allowed to go his own way, the way for which alone he may be built.

(1, 50, 2, 13, 91211) The Guru will go with the disciple in his own path and then gradually turn him into the supreme path at the ripe moment.
(1, 50, 2, 14, 91332) Suppose a car is going at top speed.
(1, 50, 2, 15, 91369) To stop it at once or to turn it at once would be attended by disastrous consequences.

(1, 51, 0, 1, 91804) Aham, all refer to the same thing.

(1, 51, 2, 4, 91985) Bhagavan spoke about the way in which in the old days he used to climb to the peak at any time he felt like it, and that by any route or even no route.
(1, 51, 2, 5, 92137) He said only the grass cutters knew some of the routes he used.

(1, 51, 2, 7, 92335) Finding me seated there, they would ask me for the route to the hill top.

(1, 51, 4, 16, 92958) Bhagavan related how, while he was living in Skandasramam, cheetahs used to visit him.
(1, 51, 4, 17, 93045) He mentioned two such instances.
(1, 51, 4, 18, 93078) In one, the cheetah made a terrific roar which clearly appeared aggressive, but it did nothing to us and went chasing monkeys.
(1, 51, 4, 19, 93205) On the other occasion, the cheetah walked up slowly and majestically and on nearing the Asramam gave a loud roar, but one in which only peace and no aggression could be detected, just as if it wanted to announce its arrival and no more.
(1, 51, 4, 20, 93442) It came very near, where the hill stream is now flowing in Skandasramam, and after a time, slowly walked away, giving another roar, as it were to announce its leave taking.

(1, 52, 0, 9, 94605) He explained that the name Skandasramam was given because the Asramam was originally planned and built by one Kandaswami, an old disciple, at great self sacrifice and by his unaided physical efforts.

(1, 52, 0, 11, 94957) After the need passes, the water also disappears. He traced the history of the hill stream now flowing in Skandasramam, how he first discovered its source further up and worked with his then disciples to lead it down.
(1, 52, 0, 12, 95175) There was a terrific storm and rain one night and it appeared to the townsmen in the morning, from that distance, as if a big road had been made overnight.
(1, 52, 0, 13, 95331) It seems during this occurrence a tank called Pada Thirtham (TR ojRm) was completely filled up and there was mud and rock heaped up to a height of about fifteen feet where the tank originally stood.
(1, 52, 0, 14, 95530) (I learnt later, on 12 12 45, from Bhagavan that half or three quarters of this tank was again restored by a devotee).
(1, 53, 0, 1, 95649) Bhagavan also narrated at length the history of the monkeys associated with his stay in Virupakshi Cave and Skandasramam, explaining in particular how Sil TVu (The lame boy, Bhagavans pet name for him) came under Bhagavans influence.
(1, 53, 0, 2, 95883) It seems, while Bhagavan was in Virupakshi Cave, a monkey had been bitten and mauled badly by the then king monkey and left for dead near the cave.
(1, 53, 0, 3, 96031) Bhagavan took pity on him and tended him and he recovered.
(1, 53, 0, 4, 96090) Afterwards, he was attached to Bhagavan and was always with him, getting his daily feed at Bhagavans Asramam.
(1, 53, 0, 5, 96200) When the other monkeys came there, the lame one would not allow them to approach Bhagavan and he pointed out to Bhagavan the king who had made him lame.
(1, 53, 0, 6, 96353) But later on, all the monkeys used to come there and used to show regard and love to their erstwhile enemy, the lame monkey.
(1, 53, 0, 7, 96478) In course of time the lame one became the monkey king.
(1, 53, 0, 8, 96533) One day Bhagavan and his party had left Skandasramam for giripradakshina, leaving the Asramam in charge of one or two who stayed behind.
(1, 53, 0, 9, 96670) During Bhagavans absence the lame one and a host of other monkeys came and broke the twigs and small branches of all the trees at Skandasramam and played havoc with all they found there.
(1, 53, 0, 10, 96857) Bhagavan was wondering why they did so.
(1, 53, 0, 11, 96897) The next day the monkeys again came and the lame one got up the highest tree, reached its highest point and shook it and then got down.
(1, 53, 0, 12, 97033) This it seems is a sign of royal precedence among the monkeys.
(1, 53, 0, 13, 97096) When Bhagavan offered food as usual to the lame one, he would not take it, but led the man offering it to where three other monkeys were sitting, and there he shared his food with them.
(1, 53, 0, 14, 97282) The three monkeys were the queens of the deposed king, who were now the lame ones queens according to their custom and usage.
(1, 53, 0, 15, 97408) Then Bhagavan knew that the lame one had become king, that the previous day they all came to have his coronation in Bhagavans presence and finding Bhagavan absent expressed their disappointment and chagrin by breaking branches, etc.
(1, 54, 0, 1, 97641) The lame one had afterwards six children by the three queens and all the six would get a morsel each from Bhagavan when he took his meal every day.
(1, 54, 0, 2, 97789) It seems the lame one misbehaved on two occasions and hurt Bhagavan by hitting him.
(1, 54, 0, 3, 97873) Each time Bhagavan thought of punishing him by denying him admission to his presence.
(1, 54, 0, 4, 97959) But finding his pet repentant, and seeing that it was only his monkeyish nature that was responsible, Bhagavan excused him.
(1, 54, 0, 5, 98083) One of the occasions was when Bhagavan took up a plate of milk intended for the monkey and was about to blow into it to make it cool, as it was too hot for the monkey.
(1, 54, 0, 6, 98251) The monkey thought that Bhagavan was taking the milk near his mouth to drink it himself.
(1, 54, 0, 7, 98340) Bhagavan said that, at Skandasramam, sometimes a peacock and serpent used to play side by side before him, the one with its tail spread out and the other with its hood raised.

(1, 56, 1, 2, 101470) I returned here last night.
(1, 56, 1, 3, 101498) Maha Vir Prasad, Chief Engineer to the U. P. Government who had been staying here for about twenty days in October and November and who went on a pilgrimage to Rameswaram and other places, is back here.
(1, 56, 1, 4, 101701) In continuation of an old question of his with reference to a certain passage in Maha Yoga, he asked Bhagavan whether it was necessary and a condition precedent for a man to watch his breathing before beginning the mental quest Who am I?
(1, 56, 2, 5, 101939) Bhagavan: All depends on a mans pakva, i.e., his aptitude and fitness.
(1, 56, 2, 6, 102010) Those who have not the mental strength to concentrate or control their mind and direct it on the quest are advised to watch their breathing, since such watching will naturally and as a matter of course lead to cessation of thought and bring the mind under control.
(1, 56, 3, 7, 102275) Breath and mind arise from the same place and when one of them is controlled, the other is also controlled.
(1, 56, 3, 8, 102383) As a matter of fact, in the quest method  which is more correctly Whence am I? and not merely Who am I?  we are not simply trying to eliminate saying we are not the body, not the senses and so on, to reach what remains as the ultimate reality, but we are trying to find whence the I thought for the ego arises within us.
(1, 56, 3, 9, 102704) The method contains within it, though implicitly and not expressly, the watching of the breath.
(1, 56, 3, 10, 102800) When we watch wherefrom the I thought, the root of all thoughts, springs, we are necessarily watching the source of breath also, as the I thought and the breath arise from the same source.
(1, 56, 4, 11, 102989) Mr. Prasad again asked whether, for controlling breath, the regular pranayama is not better in which 1:4:2 proportion for breathing in, retaining, and breathing out is prescribed.
(1, 56, 4, 12, 103169) Bhagavan replied, All those proportions, sometimes regulated not by counting but by uttering mantras, etc., are aids for controlling the mind.
(1, 56, 4, 13, 103312) That is all.
(1, 56, 4, 14, 103325) Watching the breath is also
(1, 57, 0, 1, 103353) one form of pranayama.
(1, 57, 0, 2, 103376) Retaining breath, etc., is more violent and may be harmful in some cases, e.g., when there is no proper Guru to guide the sadhak at every step and stage.
(1, 57, 0, 3, 103530) But merely watching the breath is easy and involves no risk.

(1, 57, 2, 9, 103990) The old gentleman asked Bhagavan whether one should not first go through nirvikalpa samadhi before attaining sahaja samadhi.
(1, 57, 2, 10, 104115) Bhagavan replied, When we have vikalpas and are trying to give them up, i.e., when we are still not perfected, but have to make conscious effort to keep the mind one pointed or free from thought it is nirvikalpa samadhi.
(1, 57, 2, 11, 104336) When through practice we are always in that state, not going into samadhi and coming out again, that is the sahaja state.
(1, 57, 2, 12, 104458) In sahaja one sees always oneself.
(1, 57, 2, 13, 104493) He sees the jagat as swarupa or brahmakara.
(1, 57, 2, 14, 104537) What is once the means becomes itself the goal, eventually, whatever method one follows, dhyana, jnana or bhakti.
(1, 57, 2, 15, 104651) Samadhi is another name for ourselves, for our real state.

(1, 59, 4, 11, 108065) Referring to the stanza @e YU (3rd stanza of Sri Arunachala Mahatmya in Collected Works of Ramana Maharshi) I asked Bhagavan, whether the cave mentioned in it is inside God or inside the mountain (which of course is also said to be God).
(1, 59, 4, 12, 108303) Bhagavan replied, Of course, in the context, it means the cave is inside the hill and that there, in the cave, are all enjoyments. Bhagavan added, The stanza says you are to believe that inside this hill there is a cave, which is brilliance itself or which is glorious with light, and that all enjoyments are to be found there. I also asked Bhagavan, I have read somewhere that this place is called bhoga kshetra.
(1, 59, 4, 13, 108717) I wonder what is meant thereby. Bhagavan replied, Yes, it is said so.
(1, 59, 4, 14, 108787) But what does it mean?
(1, 59, 4, 15, 108810) If thinking of this kshetra can itself give mukti, what wonder if this place can give all other enjoyments one may desire? Dr. Srinivasa Rao asked whether
(1, 60, 0, 1, 108965) after putting oneself the question Who am I? one should remain quiet or whether one should go on to give the answer, such as I am not this body, senses, etc. or whether one should go on repeating the question Who am I?
(1, 60, 1, 2, 109184) Bhagavan: Why should you go on repeating Who am I? as if it is a mantra.
(1, 60, 1, 3, 109257) If other thoughts arise, then the questions, To whom do these thoughts arise?, Whence does the I to which these thoughts come arise? have to be asked, i.e., to keep away other thoughts.
(1, 60, 1, 4, 109443) Even in mantra japam, when the man fails to repeat the mantra, i.e., when other thoughts begin to occupy his mind, he reminds himself I have left off the mantra and begins repeating it.
(1, 60, 1, 5, 109629) The object in all paths is to keep off all other thoughts except the thought of God or Self.
(1, 60, 2, 6, 109722) In this connection Bhagavan again said The Name is God, and quoted the Bible, In the beginning was the word, and the word was with God, and the word was God. Swami Ramdas often preaches the importance of Nama smarana, the Name he uses being Sri Ram Jai Ram Jai Jai Ram.

(1, 60, 4, 9, 110144) The September 1937 number of Vision contains an article on the Philosophy of the Divine Name according to Nam Dev.
(1, 60, 4, 10, 110259) Bhagavan frequently refers to it with approval.
(1, 60, 4, 11, 110307) Today also Dr. Srinivasa Rao brought it and Bhagavan explained it to him.
(1, 60, 4, 12, 110381) Bhagavan remarked, All this must have been uttered by Nam Dev after he attained complete realisation, after touching the feet of Vishopakesar to whom Vithoba sent him for enlightenment. Bhagavan then related the following story:
(1, 60, 5, 13, 110610) Vithoba found Nam Dev had not yet realised the Supreme Truth and wanted to teach him.
(1, 60, 5, 14, 110696) When Jnaneswar and Nam Dev returned from their pilgrimage, Gora Kumbhar gave a feast to
(1, 61, 0, 1, 110784) all the saints in his place and among them were Jnaneswar and Nam Dev.
(1, 61, 0, 2, 110855) At the feast, Jnaneswar in collusion with Gora, told Gora publicly, You are a potter, daily engaged in making pots and testing them to see which are properly baked and which are not.
(1, 61, 0, 3, 111038) These pots before you (i.e., the saints) are the pots of Brahma.
(1, 61, 0, 4, 111103) See which of these are sound and which not. Thereupon Gora said, Yes Swami, I shall do so, and took up the stick with which he used to tap his pots to test their soundness; and holding it aloft in his hand he went to each of his guests and tapped each on the head as he usually did to his pots.
(1, 61, 0, 5, 111398) Each guest humbly submitted to such tapping.
(1, 61, 0, 6, 111443) But when Gora approached Nam Dev, the latter indignantly called out: You potter, what do you mean by coming to tap me with that stick? Gora thereupon told Jnaneswar, Swami, all the other pots have been properly baked.
(1, 61, 0, 7, 111661) This one, (i.e., Nam Dev) alone is not yet properly baked. All the assembled guests burst into laughter.
(1, 61, 0, 8, 111766) Nam Dev felt greatly humiliated and ran up to Vitthal with whom he was on the most intimate terms, playing with him, eating with him, sleeping with him, and so on.
(1, 61, 0, 9, 111930) Nam Dev complained of this humiliation which had happened to him, the closest friend and companion of Vitthal.
(1, 61, 0, 10, 112041) Vitthal (who of course knew all this) pretended to sympathise with him, asked for all the details of the happenings at Goras house and after hearing everything said, Why should you not have kept quiet and submitted to the tapping, as all the others did?
(1, 61, 0, 11, 112295) That is why all this trouble has come. Thereupon Nam Dev cried all the more and said, You also want to join the others and humiliate me.
(1, 61, 0, 12, 112432) Why should I have submitted like the others?
(1, 61, 0, 13, 112477) Am I not your closest friend, your child? Vitthal said, You have not yet properly understood the truth.
(1, 61, 0, 14, 112581) And you wont understand if I tell you.
(1, 61, 0, 15, 112620) But go to the saint who is in a ruined temple in such and such a forest.
(1, 61, 0, 16, 112693) He will be able to give you enlightenment. Nam Dev accordingly went there and found an old, unassuming man sleeping in a corner of the temple with his feet on a Siva lingam.
(1, 61, 0, 17, 112867) Nam Dev could hardly believe this was the man from
(1, 62, 0, 1, 112918) whom he  the companion of Vitthal  was to gain enlightenment.
(1, 62, 0, 2, 112980) However, as there was none else there, Nam Dev went near the man and clapped his hands.
(1, 62, 0, 3, 113068) The old man woke up with a start and, seeing Nam Dev, said, Oh, you are Nam Dev whom Vitthal has sent here.
(1, 62, 0, 4, 113176) Come! Nam Dev was dumb founded and began to think, This must be a great man. Still he thought it was revolting that any man, however great, should be resting his feet on a lingam.
(1, 62, 0, 5, 113356) He asked the old man, You seem to be a great personage.
(1, 62, 0, 6, 113412) But is it proper for you to have your feet on a lingam? The old man replied, Oh, are my feet on a lingam?
(1, 62, 0, 7, 113518) Where is it?
(1, 62, 0, 8, 113531) Please remove my feet elsewhere. Nam Dev removed the feet and put them in various places.
(1, 62, 0, 9, 113621) Wherever they were put, there was a Siva lingam.
(1, 62, 0, 10, 113670) Finally, he took them on his lap and he himself became a Siva lingam.
(1, 62, 0, 11, 113740) Then he realised the truth and the old gentleman said, Now you can go back. Bhagavan added, It is to be noted that only when he surrendered himself, and touched the feet of his guru, enlightenment came.
(1, 62, 0, 12, 113943) After this final enlightenment Nam Dev returned to his house and for some days did not go to Vitthal at the temple, though it had been his habit not only to visit Vitthal every day, but to spend most of his time with Vitthal at the temple.
(1, 62, 0, 13, 114183) So after a few days, Vitthal went to Nam Devs house and like a guileless soul enquired how it was that Nam Dev has forgotten him and never visited him.
(1, 62, 0, 14, 114335) Nam Dev replied, No more of your fooling me.
(1, 62, 0, 15, 114380) I know now.
(1, 62, 0, 16, 114392) Where is the place where you are not!
(1, 62, 0, 17, 114430) To be with you, should I go to the temple?
(1, 62, 0, 18, 114473) Do I exist apart from you? Then Vitthal said, So you now understand the truth.
(1, 62, 0, 19, 114552) That is why you had to be sent for this final lesson.
(1, 62, 1, 20, 114606) Bhagavan referred to the article in the Vision of December, 1945 on Sthita Prajna and to the lines from Sat Darshana quoted in that article.
(1, 62, 1, 21, 114747) Dr. Syed thereupon asked Bhagavan when Reality in Forty Verses was made by Bhagavan.
(1, 62, 1, 22, 114832) Bhagavan said, It was recently something like 1928. Muruganar has noted down
(1, 63, 0, 1, 114909) somewhere the different dates.
(1, 63, 0, 2, 114940) One day Muruganar said that some stray verses composed by me now and then on various occasions should not be allowed to die, but should be collected together and some more added to them to bring the whole number to forty, and that the entire forty should be made into a book with a proper title.
(1, 63, 0, 3, 115236) He accordingly gathered about thirty or less stanzas and requested me to make the rest to bring the total to forty.
(1, 63, 0, 4, 115352) I did so, composing a few stanzas on different occasions as the mood came upon me.
(1, 63, 0, 5, 115435) When the number came up to forty, Muruganar went about deleting one after another of the old collection of thirty or less on the pretext they were not quite germane to the subject on hand or otherwise not quite suitable, and requesting me to make fresh ones in place of the deleted ones.
(1, 63, 0, 6, 115723) When this process was over, and there were forty stanzas as required by Muruganar, I found that in the forty there were but two stanzas out of the old ones and all the rest had been newly composed.
(1, 63, 0, 7, 115921) It was not made according to any set scheme, nor at a stretch, nor systematically.
(1, 63, 0, 8, 116004) I composed different stanzas on different occasions and Muruganar and others afterwards arranged them in some order according to the thoughts expressed in them to give some appearance of connected and regular treatment of the subject, viz., Reality. (The stanzas contained in the old collection and deleted by Muruganar were about twenty.
(1, 63, 0, 9, 116343) These were afterwards added as supplement to the above work and the Supplement too now contains 40 verses).
(1, 63, 1, 10, 116451) Bhagavan also said, Marital Garland of Letters was composed partly at the Virupakshi Cave and partly on my walks round the hill.
(1, 63, 1, 11, 116580) Upadesa Saram alone was written with a scheme and a set purpose and at a stretch at one sitting.
(1, 63, 1, 12, 116677) That was because Muruganar had set a limit of one hundred stanzas for the entire theme of the poem and in it had planned that the particular subject of Upadesa should be confined to thirty stanzas, of which again he had already composed three stanzas, leaving
(1, 64, 0, 1, 116937) thus only twenty seven stanzas within the brief compass of which I was required to deal with the whole subject.
(1, 64, 1, 2, 117049) In the afternoon Bhagavan explained to Dr. Srinivasa Rao the significance of the name Rama.
(1, 64, 1, 3, 117141) The Ra stands for the Self and ma for the ego.
(1, 64, 1, 4, 117188) As one goes on repeating Rama, Rama, the ma disappears, getting merged in the Ra and then Ra alone remains.
(1, 64, 1, 5, 117296) In that state there is no conscious effort at dhyana, but it is there, for dhyana is our real nature.

(1, 64, 6, 12, 118037) When I went to the Pathasala to bring some Sanskrit books at Bhagavans instance, an incident occurred in the hall which is recorded below.
(1, 64, 6, 13, 118176) Miss Kamdin (now Mrs. McIver) was sitting next to a lady who went into a trance and purported to have received sugar candy from Narayana i.e., God.
(1, 64, 6, 14, 118324) She thinks that there was no trickery at all in it and that the candy came mysteriously into the ladys hands.

(1, 64, 7, 16, 118471) The incident is thus recorded by Mr. K.A. Mahatani:
(1, 64, 8, 17, 118523) On 8th December 1945 at about 3 45 p.m., I saw a Gujarati widow aged about 50 years sitting in the hall with two girls of
(1, 65, 0, 1, 118645) about eight and ten years in the front row on the side reserved for ladies.
(1, 65, 0, 2, 118721) I could see that she was in samadhi.
(1, 65, 1, 3, 118758) After a few minutes she fell on her back quietly in samadhi posture without stretching her legs.
(1, 65, 1, 4, 118855) People in the hall thought that she was in a hysterical fit, but those two girls said that she was in samadhi and that it often happened to her.
(1, 65, 1, 5, 119000) Then those two girls started singing a Gujarati bhajan song in a nice tune.
(1, 65, 1, 6, 119076) (The song begins Haji mare gher thaya lila ler).
(1, 65, 2, 7, 119125) Bhagavan was all the time attentively looking at the lady and enjoying the tune, keeping time by beating his knee with his hand.
(1, 65, 2, 8, 119254) I had never before noticed Bhagavan so interested in any song.
(1, 65, 3, 9, 119317) After a few minutes the lady got up without anybodys help and sat in samadhi in the same posture, and began to mutter something in Gujarati.
(1, 65, 3, 10, 119458) I asked her whether she saw any vision.
(1, 65, 3, 11, 119498) She said in Hindustani Yes, and added that Sri Narayan gave a message for all in the hall.
(1, 65, 3, 12, 119589) Let them close their eyes, be still, without any pravritti (i.e., without any thought or action) and I will give darshan to one and all.
(1, 65, 4, 13, 119726) I translated to Bhagavan what she said.
(1, 65, 4, 14, 119766) For two or three minutes she was bowing her head with hands clasped towards Bhagavan.
(1, 65, 4, 15, 119852) Then we noticed that she handed over a piece of sugar candy to one of the two girls sitting near her.
(1, 65, 5, 16, 119954) Before that we had seen her open hands quite empty.
(1, 65, 5, 17, 120006) I asked the girl what was that sugar candy and how it came into her hands.
(1, 65, 6, 18, 120081) The girl replied, This is prasad given to the lady by Bhagavan Narayan; and whenever she goes into samadhi she gets different things (such as fruit, flowers, garlands, sandal, kumkum and sweets) on different occasions.
(1, 65, 7, 19, 120300) Then the lady said that she used to get such moods since she was six years old.
(1, 65, 7, 20, 120380) The girl presented the piece of sugar
(1, 66, 0, 1, 120418) candy to Bhagavan.
